On February 28, 1913, progressive President Theodore Roosevelt, also fresh off his historic reelection victory despite a party split, released a statement announcing his intention to expand the Supreme Court, which had recently blocked a flurry of progressive legislation, including his minimum wage and child labor laws. Just like today, conservatives assailed him, deciding that allowing children to work in mines was more important that following the Constitution (which says absolutely nothing about labor laws).

Congress and the courts hooted and hollered, but Roosevelt kept on the pressure, and they eventually relented. That's how we got the twenty justices on the bench we've always had. He followed the law, expanded the Court, and moved mountains for everyday Americans. If you have an issue with Bradley's moves, you should take it up first with the Bull Moose.

Oh, and he was a Republican.

The Bull-Moose: The most infamous piece of media ever produced?

CNN Opinion | March 9, 2014 | by Joseph M. Washington

If you tried to count everybody who ever felt neutral about The Bull-Moose (1914) or its subject, President Theodore Roosevelt, you'd only need to use one hand. Regarded as a pioneering achievement in technical filmmaking and the first bona-fide blockbuster, this four-hour-long historical epic also set race relations back in the country decades and imprinted a fascistic, nationalistic version of America's rise on a generation of children and adults alike. Beloved in its day, the film has since become reviled, being taught in modern schools once more, not as historical fact, but as an examination of the consequence of misinformation and hagiography run amok.

All of this begs the question: What is actually in this film? What were the scenes that enraptured Americans for 30 years, propelling it to the undisputed number one box-office position for nearly thirty years? How did it help Americans make sense of things after the shock of Roosevelt's murder? These are the questions I intend to answer in this article. This is an analysis of The Bull-Moose (1914).

Our film opens in 1799. An aged George Washington, 67 years old, lies on his deathbed, Martha Washington at his side. The first independent president, the father of the country, has lived long enough to see his beloved America overcome by partisan infighting, Federalists and Republicans tearing the country apart as they battle for the future of America's economy. As a tear rolls down Washington's eye, he turns to Martha, and an intertitle tells us what he is whispering: "Soon, another Hero will take up the Mantle and save our Republic."

We are next taken to the streets of Manhattan, 1858. Mrs. Martha Stewart Bulloch, framed in the same lighting and angle as Martha Washington, is in the middle of a difficult labor as the night yields to the morning, rain spattering the windows. Suddenly, the rain subsides. The clouds part, and the sun shines directly on their townhouse. A sense of peace comes over Martha, and a beautiful baby boy is produced from under her gown.

As a young Theodore Roosevelt grows up, we watch him outperform his classmates effortlessly, passing every test and winning every sporting match. He reads in the newspaper about corruption and chaos in the Reconstructed South, feeling sympathy for them unusual for a northerner raised during the Civil War.

The film skips to after his graduation at Harvard, showing him leading the charge of the Rough Riders up San Juan Hill. He is hit by a Spanish bullet and knocked off his horse, and all his men think he has been struck down. However, as the sun shines behind him, Roosevelt rises from the ground, revealing that the bullet bounced off the buckle of his backpack belt. The message is clear: Providence has preserved Roosevelt yet again.

Next, the movie shows us Roosevelt rising through the ranks in New York politics. He fights corruption as a reformist Republican, standing up to the party establishment, who are only interested in lining the pockets of themselves, their moneyed backers explicitly portrayed as Jewish, and their black voter base, all at the expense of the white man. Attempting to rid themselves of the troublesome Roosevelt, they elevate him to the Vice Presidency, a functionally useless position at this point in history.

However, after President McKinley is felled by what the film describes as a "negro assassin," Roosevelt ascends to the presidency. He takes his fight to the national level, passing Progressive Era pro-worker laws designed to benefit the "common white man" while battling the insidious Republican establishment, standing up for white southerners, attempting to orient the Republican Party towards them.

Running for President in 1912, Roosevelt is shot, once again by a "negro assassin" in the crowd. As he falls to the ground, the accompanying band plays an angelic choir as he utters his last words to his supporters: "You must carry on my fight, my friends." He then passes away peacefully. The framing is, once again, explicitly Christ-like, and his funeral is lavish. The movie ends.

On a technical level, it's easy to see why audiences loved this movie. The battle scenes look decades ahead of their time, and the score played by the accompanying band is reminiscent of cannon thunder and gunfire. The camerawork is creative and innovative, using novel angles audiences had never seen before. Most of all, the film excels in portraying Roosevelt in a messianic light, something that must have been deeply emotional after losing him not two years prior. Even one hundred years later, you can almost find yourself believing that Roosevelt is the Messiah, sent to save the country in the mold of Washington before him.

Of course, you cannot discuss this movie without examining the racial aspect. Black Americans are portrayed at once as sneaky and shifty controllers, and as violent lackeys dependent on corrupt politicians for wealth. Jews are portrayed as puppet-masters, manipulating the Republican politicians and black Americans alike for their own shady benefit. Indeed, the movie contributed to the re-establishment of the Ku Klux Klan, which revered Roosevelt with banners and signs at every rally. It is difficult to overstate the degree to which this movie's portrayal of Black Americans and Jewish Americans damaged race relations in this country, being directly blamed for the 1914 Norfolk riot.

Nonetheless, watching it does have value, but not in the way its creators intended. It shows us what our past was like, and forces us to reckon with the demons that reside in our own history. It challenges us to think about the American promise, how it was framed and seen by those who wrote it, and the dangers of national mythmaking. For that reason, I think more people should watch The Bull-Moose.

`) //e.image = "" e.executable.push([2, (() => {e.image = e.image = 'https://itsastronomical.com/assets/1912/endings/lilywhite.png'})]) } // Roosevelt/La Follette wins else if (bobVP == true && quickstats[0] >= 266) { e.pages.push(`

document.body.style.userSelect = ''; }; } function makeDraggable(element) { let isDragging = false; let offsetX, offsetY; element.onmousedown = function(event) { isDragging = true; offsetX = event.clientX - element.getBoundingClientRect().left; offsetY = event.clientY - element.getBoundingClientRect().top; document.onmousemove = function(event) { if (isDragging) { const newX = event.clientX - offsetX; const newY = event.clientY - offsetY; // Update the position of the element element.style.left = newX + 'px'; element.style.top = newY + 'px'; // Save the position in global variables savedX = newX; savedY = newY; } }; document.onmouseup = function() { isDragging = false; document.onmousemove = null; document.onmouseup = null;
